  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_39345360-81a6-4fb0-96e6-1b39fb1d75a7'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);

  <span id="freeText8482430696641549821" style="display:none"><div>For 4,000 years, the lavish crypt of the Pharaoh Mamose has never been found...until the Seventh Scroll, a cryptic message written by he slave Taita, gives beautiful Egyptologist Royan Al Simma a tantalizing clue to its location.<br /><br />But this is a treasure cache others would kill to possess. Only one step ahead of assassins, Royan runs for her life and into the arms of the only man she can trust, Sir Nicholas Quenton-Harper-a daring man who will stake his fortune and his life to join her hunt for the king's tomb. Together, they will embark on a breathtaking journey to the most exotic locale on earth, where the greatest mystery of ancient Egypt, a chilling danger and an explosive passion are waiting.<br /><br />Steeped in ancient mystery, drama and action, <i>The Seventh Scroll </i>is a masterpiece from a storyteller at the height of his powers.<br /></div></span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3015110664" />
    <a title="Henry Avila" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5431458-henry-avila"><img alt="Henry Avila" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1307597403p2/5431458.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3015110664?book_show_action=true">Oct 01, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Henry Avila" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Henry Avila" href="/user/show/5431458-henry-avila">Henry Avila</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3015110664" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8883035572096209440">A few years ago a little more or less than 4,000, a ruler the pharaoh Mamose (fictitious) of ancient Egypt flees for his life, invaders the ruthless Hyksos from the north drive him from his throne, so he travels south.This was from the book River God by Wilbur Smith, the sequel set in the modern (1995) as archaelogists a very loose term maybe a better one is grave robbers trying to find his lost tomb hidden in a remote section of Ethiopia.The unbelievable riches is quite staggering... Countless </span>
  <span id="freeText8883035572096209440" style="display:none">A few years ago a little more or less than 4,000, a ruler the pharaoh Mamose (fictitious) of ancient Egypt flees for his life, invaders the ruthless Hyksos from the north drive him from his throne, so he travels south.This was from the book River God by Wilbur Smith, the sequel set in the modern (1995) as archaelogists a very loose term maybe a better one is grave robbers trying to find his lost tomb hidden in a remote section of Ethiopia.The unbelievable riches is quite staggering... Countless millions of dollars however who owns it? Ethiopia or Egypt or finders keepers as the thieves from the past are reincarnated but not for the best. Sir Nicholas Quenton-Harper a British aristocrat of minor heritage that makes Indiana Jones seem like an amateur which he is technically having no degree except avarice and this is a common vice which sadly is the majority in the world, he has cash flow problems like many. It would overflow both banks of the mighty Nile River in volume, to ad interest an Egyptologist half English the other part Egyptian, Dr. Rogan Al Summa don't have to state a beauty with divided loyalty working for the government in Cairo. The seventh scroll she and her late husband discovered written by the genius Taita ( self described) a slave and eunuch but has ambitions. Clues are on the papyrus yet the amused writer is fun loving his cryptic words hard to understand, deciphering quiet a challenge. And is determined to stop thiefs by ingenius traps that kill the dishonorable who desecrate the sacred resting place of his master. A German billionaire Herr von Schiller is also after the prize and murder not important as the circle tightens, his hand nears ...butchering can be profitable. A motley group for good or evil are involved in the actions. While intense scenes of carnage spills the pages red the author is fearless in perpetuating the adnormal and offending everyone, to be honest. Nevertheless interest remains high as the inevitable lovers near their goal in a laberinth under the Blue Nile...will the waters crush them? How easily this can occur. Hazards around every corner the dark brings the nightmares, the dangers can strike at anytime, there is something about being underground, giving the imagination the full throttle, an unlit scream you can never forget, feel very apprehensive , weak and claustraphobic. The second in the series, so far six, the ancient Egyptian civilization the most intriguing of all. An imaginitive story which gives the reader a picture , a piece, a peak in the land of the Nile, always flowing by in her perpetual wanderings , may it never cease. And archaelogy be viewed as an art for knowledge not a treasure hunt...</span>
  <a data-text-id="8883035572096209440" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">

<span id="freeTextContainer3858178873293107044">There are moments that I feel like reading something light and entertaining. What could be better than an adventure-treasure hunting suspense? That is exactly what <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4043.Wilbur_Smith' title='Wilbur Smith' rel='nofollow noopener'>Wilbur Smith</a>, a masterful writer of such stories, created in <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/416580.The_Seventh_Scroll__Ancient_Egypt___2_' title='The Seventh Scroll (Ancient Egypt, #2) by Wilbur Smith' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Seventh Scroll</a>. It is about the archaeological quest and discovery of the lost Pharaohs Mamose's tomb. The action sets off swiftly in Egypt moves to England to end up in Ethiopia. There are very believable and engaging characters, good and honorable or totally evil. For </span>
  <span id="freeText3858178873293107044" style="display:none">There are moments that I feel like reading something light and entertaining. What could be better than an adventure-treasure hunting suspense? That is exactly what <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4043.Wilbur_Smith" title="Wilbur Smith" rel="nofollow noopener">Wilbur Smith</a>, a masterful writer of such stories, created in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/416580.The_Seventh_Scroll__Ancient_Egypt___2_" title="The Seventh Scroll (Ancient Egypt, #2) by Wilbur Smith" rel="nofollow noopener">The Seventh Scroll</a>. It is about the archaeological quest and discovery of the lost Pharaohs Mamose's tomb. The action sets off swiftly in Egypt moves to England to end up in Ethiopia. There are very believable and engaging characters, good and honorable or totally evil. For those looking for a good thriller, read it and enjoy.</span>

<div id="review_15570905" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/15570905" />
    <a title="Werner" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/903390-werner"><img alt="Werner" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1216337931p2/903390.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/15570905?book_show_action=true">Jun 23, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Werner" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Werner" href="/user/show/903390-werner">Werner</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Indiana Jones fans; adventure fans generally</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/903390-werner?shelf=action-adventure">action-adventure</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer15570905" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15743025178808348944">In the 1994 opening novel of his Ancient Egypt series, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/429138.River_God__Ancient_Egypt___1_' title='River God (Ancient Egypt, #1) by Wilbur Smith' rel='nofollow noopener'>River God</a>, (which I haven't read) veteran South African novelist Wilbur Smith depicts the fictional Pharaoh Mamose finding a temporary refuge, with some of his people, from the invading Hyskos in the valley of Ethiopia's Blue Nile --a region that's wild and largely unexplored even in modern times. This novel presupposes the events of the earlier one; but although it's billed as the second book of the series, it takes place nearly 4,000 years</span>
  <span id="freeText15743025178808348944" style="display:none">In the 1994 opening novel of his Ancient Egypt series, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/429138.River_God__Ancient_Egypt___1_" title="River God (Ancient Egypt, #1) by Wilbur Smith" rel="nofollow noopener">River God</a>, (which I haven't read) veteran South African novelist Wilbur Smith depicts the fictional Pharaoh Mamose finding a temporary refuge, with some of his people, from the invading Hyskos in the valley of Ethiopia's Blue Nile --a region that's wild and largely unexplored even in modern times. This novel presupposes the events of the earlier one; but although it's billed as the second book of the series, it takes place nearly 4,000 years later, in the author's present, and can be read as a stand-alone (any information about the earlier events that the reader needs is supplied in the text of this book).<br /><br />While excavating the tomb of Mamose's queen Lostris, archaeologists Duraid al-Simma and his beautiful (and much younger) Anglo-Egyptian wife Royan have unearthed ten scrolls written by the queen's scribe Taita. The seventh scroll provides clues to Mamose's Ethiopian resting place, with its treasures. But knowledge of these clues has fallen into the hands of a ruthless, wealthy and powerful antiquities collector who's not about to let minor things like laws against murder and theft keep him from adding this loot to his private collection. When Duraid is killed early on, and the scroll stolen, Royan must follow his dying instructions by joining forces with another wealthy collector, widowed adventurer Sir Nicholas Quentin-Harper (who's much closer to her age) to find the tomb before her husband's killers do. There's a complication, though. Royan wants to turn the treasure over to the proper authorities. But while Nicholas, like Royan, wants himself and her to be first at the tomb, and is ethical enough (unlike their competition) to not hurt any innocents --and chivalrous enough to protect Royan as gallantly as she could wish-- he has ambitions to add the find to HIS collection. So our heroine and hero here are working partly as allies --but partly at cross purposes, too, which makes for an interesting battle of wits. (And Cupid may complicate things, too....)<br /><br />Smith's reputation is as a popular novelist of action-adventure fiction, much of it set in his native Africa, delivering excitement and suspense. I've never read any of his other work, except for a <i>Reader's Digest</i> condensed version of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/74778.The_Sunbird" title="The Sunbird by Wilbur Smith" rel="nofollow noopener">The Sunbird</a>, but this (his 25th novel) would suggest that his reputation is deserved. He also provides convincing characters, a well-constructed plot, and realistic (sometimes wryly humorous) dialogue. Where appropriate, his descriptions are vivid, and he has an obviously considerable knowledge of African history, culture and geography, which he draws on to enrich the book. In the best tradition of action-adventure fiction, he views his characters' actions in terms of a moral code that accurately distinguishes between good and evil. (While religion isn't a major theme here, Royan and some other characters are Coptic Christians who take their faith seriously, and Smith treats it respectfully.)<br /><br />As Smith depicts him, Nicholas had (before his marriage) a womanizing past, which he appears to be furnished with in order to establish his virility credentials, in the misguided assumption that this will make him more swoon-worthy to female readers. (If they have much sense, it won't!) But despite what movie raters would describe as "some sensuality," the central romantic plot here is clean, and the only explicit sexual content involves the villain. (This is disgusting, but it's intended to be.) There's some profanity in the book, but not a lot, and no obscenity that I can recall. Some of the violence is extensive and gruesome (and my oldest daughter, who's read more of Smith's work than I have, reports that this is characteristic), but it was nothing I couldn't handle.<br /><br />Most fans of action-adventure, IMO, would find this right up their alley! It also has the advantage of essentially being a stand-alone, and not sucking the reader into a potentially long series.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15743025178808348944" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer4916535635222870546">Abandoned at page 313. I made the mistake of taking a break and after picking the book up again I simply could not care less about what happens. It's not badly written and I'm sure the clues to the pharao's untouched grave might be more fun if I had read "River Gods". As it is, there is just so much repetition I just can't take anymore. In fact, this book has put me entirely off reading, which rarely happens. I hardly know what to do with myself! I don't expect this feeling to last longer than m</span>
  <span id="freeText4916535635222870546" style="display:none">Abandoned at page 313. I made the mistake of taking a break and after picking the book up again I simply could not care less about what happens. It's not badly written and I'm sure the clues to the pharao's untouched grave might be more fun if I had read "River Gods". As it is, there is just so much repetition I just can't take anymore. In fact, this book has put me entirely off reading, which rarely happens. I hardly know what to do with myself! I don't expect this feeling to last longer than maybe 20 minutes - which is how long I need to cook dinner - but it's quite horrific nonetheless.</span>

<div id="review_119808759" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/119808759" />
    <a title="Benjamin Thomas" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4209082-benjamin-thomas"><img alt="Benjamin Thomas" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1451660582p2/4209082.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/119808759?book_show_action=true">Sep 02, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Benjamin Thomas" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Benjamin Thomas" href="/user/show/4209082-benjamin-thomas">Benjamin Thomas</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780312945985" href="/book/show/1908853.The_Seventh_Scroll">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4209082-benjamin-thomas?shelf=thriller-historical">thriller-historical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4209082-benjamin-thomas?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer119808759" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13431700193894062572">"The Seventh Scroll", by Wilbur Smith is the follow-up to "River God", one of the best historical novels I have read in a very long time. "The Seventh Scroll" was meant to be read after "River God" but it is not exactly a sequel. It actually takes place in the present day with a couple of archeologist types who are out to discover the hidden tombs that were depicted in the first book. What an intriguing concept! What we got to live through before is now seen through the eyes of history, includin</span>
  <span id="freeText13431700193894062572" style="display:none">"The Seventh Scroll", by Wilbur Smith is the follow-up to "River God", one of the best historical novels I have read in a very long time. "The Seventh Scroll" was meant to be read after "River God" but it is not exactly a sequel. It actually takes place in the present day with a couple of archeologist types who are out to discover the hidden tombs that were depicted in the first book. What an intriguing concept! What we got to live through before is now seen through the eyes of history, including all the distortions of history that are bound to happen. Afterall, what we assume to be true through archeological research isn't necessarily the way it really happened. <br /><br />The author does another intriguing thing in this book. He inserts himself into his own fiction, having a main character refer to Wilbur Smith as having authored the fictional "River God" from information uncovered in the first 6 scrolls found in the tomb. Of course it is the 7th scroll that the characters are after in this second book. This leads to some funny moments as the two main protagonists argue about just how accurate Wilbur Smith was in the first book; one of them goes so far as to dismiss Mr Smith as a hack writer who changes the historical record in order to include more sex and violence. It's a twisted loop whereby an author actually negatively critques his own work...I guess you have to be pretty secure in your own writing career to do that. Regardless, it works wonderfully here. <br /><br />The book itself was only slightly less enjoyable than the first. When you get right down to it, this is a pretty straight forward adventure/treasure hunting story along the likes of "Romancing the Stone". But Wilbur Smith is an outstanding writer who has a knack for making you read just a little more and then still more despite any deadlines you may have to deal with. You know the two main characters will hook up by the end but its not predictable how that happens. The dangerous scenes in the book are really dangerous and the suspense is spot on. As for historical accuracy...everything sure seemed to be accurate based on my limited knowledge of ancient Egypt and my trip to that country a few years ago. I hadn't planned on reading this series this year but now I am already looking forward to the third book, "Warlock", where we get to return to the ancient times and see what happens next! <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13431700193894062572" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer5052751373174528029">By now, I have read this book about 4 times which tells you how much I loved it. Smith has written a beautiful, adventerous story about love, greed, death and courage. It takes place in a few different places, starting in Egypt, moving its way to England and then to Ethiopia. Wilbur Smith created wonderful characters, both lovable and detestable.   <span id="freeText5052751373174528029" style="display:none">By now, I have read this book about 4 times which tells you how much I loved it. Smith has written a beautiful, adventerous story about love, greed, death and courage. It takes place in a few different places, starting in Egypt, moving its way to England and then to Ethiopia. Wilbur Smith created wonderful characters, both lovable and detestable. The basic plot of the story is that a tomb found in Egypt reveals seven scrolls, the last scroll containing cryptic clues to the burial site of an ancient king. Two people come together, one a woman from Egypt and a man from England, and struggle against the power and corruption of a lunatic multi-millionare to find the burial site and the treasures that lie within. After reading most of Smiths books to date, I have to say that this is one of my favourite. I really enjoyed this book. Read it; you will not be sorry.</span>

<div id="review_343834159" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/343834159" />
    <a title="Sunny" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4214583-sunny"><img alt="Sunny" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1294879781p2/4214583.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/343834159?book_show_action=true">Jun 06, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sunny" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sunny" href="/user/show/4214583-sunny">Sunny</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer343834159" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16752335556028336536">I'm not even sure where I got this book from - I think it might have been out of a bag of books that my mom gave me. I'd never heard of the author before, and didn't even realize it was part of a series until I looked it up on Goodreads when I was about halfway through the novel. But even though The Seventh Scroll is actually the second book in a series, it works as a stand alone novel.<br/><br/>Mostly, I enjoyed The Seventh Scroll. The character of Royan was entertaining to read and easy to relate to, fo</span>
  <span id="freeText16752335556028336536" style="display:none">I'm not even sure where I got this book from - I think it might have been out of a bag of books that my mom gave me. I'd never heard of the author before, and didn't even realize it was part of a series until I looked it up on Goodreads when I was about halfway through the novel. But even though The Seventh Scroll is actually the second book in a series, it works as a stand alone novel.<br /><br />Mostly, I enjoyed The Seventh Scroll. The character of Royan was entertaining to read and easy to relate to, for the most part. Overall it's a decent story, and parts of it were fascinating. Many other parts were boring, dull, poorly written, overly descriptive, super predictable, and a few were actually cringe-worthy. And something that bothered me immensely, the POV changes randomly, sometimes even in the middle of a paragraph. I love multiple Points of View, but I'm of the opinion that the POV change needs to happen at a chapter break, or at least a section break within a chapter. But in The Seventh Scroll, the POV seems to change completely randomly, and didn't always makes sense to me.<br /><br />As I read through the many reviews from other people, though, I almost wonder if I'm missing something. Many others seem to love the author and this series, and rave on and on about how good it is. I didn't hate The Seventh Scroll, but I didn't love it either. It's a good read, and starts well, but the middle part of the novel loses momentum and never quite picks it back up again. By the time I muddled through to the end I just wanted them to find the treasure and be done already.<br /><br />I'm giving The Seventh Scroll two stars. I feel vaguely bad about doing so, but I'm also not quite willing to go to three stars and say I liked it. It was ok. I probably won't try any of the rest of the series, even though the first book in the series, River God, seems to get rave reviews. But I think I'll pass, for now at least.<br /><br />Have you read The Seventh Scroll by Wilbur Smith? What did you think - am I way off base? <br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://sunnyreads.blogspot.com">http://sunnyreads.blogspot.com</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="16752335556028336536" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer4837093603578943424">BOOK FAIL - <br/>OK. I don't have a LOT of quirks when it comes to my reading habits. If it's interesting and holds my attention I'm pretty forgiving about a lot of things that might annoy another reader.<br/><br/>But my ONE unforgivable, throw the book across the room, stomp on it, then vow (out loud and with MUCH vehemence) to NEVER NEVER NEVER read that author again is when an author references themselves and/or one of their other books within the context of the plot of the book I'm reading.<br/><br/>Which this jacka</span>
  <span id="freeText4837093603578943424" style="display:none">BOOK FAIL - <br />OK. I don't have a LOT of quirks when it comes to my reading habits. If it's interesting and holds my attention I'm pretty forgiving about a lot of things that might annoy another reader.<br /><br />But my ONE unforgivable, throw the book across the room, stomp on it, then vow (out loud and with MUCH vehemence) to NEVER NEVER NEVER read that author again is when an author references themselves and/or one of their other books within the context of the plot of the book I'm reading.<br /><br />Which this jackass did THREE TIMES in the first 20 pages of the book. The first two I was almost ready to let it go, but the third time he actually made it seem as if the characters in his NOVEL had interacted with him on the writing of the previous book, and they were then TALKING about him as if HE were a character and using his first novel as a REFERENCE.<br /><br />Now look, I am HAPPY to read about continuous worlds. Hell, Louise Erdrich is one of my absolutely favorite authors and her books stalk entire lineages, reference previous plots, and often require a flow chart.<br /><br />But seriously - AUTHORS DO NOT ENTER YOUR FICTIONAL WORLD. You are DESTROYING the fiction when you do this! And it's arrogant! And it's just a JERK FUCKING MOVE. DON'T DO IT DON'T DO IT DON'T DO IT.<br /><br />I feel strongly about this.<br /><br />Just. Don't.<br /><br />So yeah. This book has been duly thrown, stomped, and cursed. And Wilbur Smith can bite me.</span>

<div id="review_1246541934" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1246541934" />
    <a title="Alan Veale" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/41773003-alan-veale"><img alt="Alan Veale" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442955122p2/41773003.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1246541934?book_show_action=true">Apr 05, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alan Veale" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alan Veale" href="/user/show/41773003-alan-veale">Alan Veale</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1246541934" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18402071591851339067">I was first introduced to Wilbur Smith’s writing several years ago while laid up with my leg in plaster halfway through a holiday in Lanzarote. I needed something to read, and there were several paperbacks left behind in our rented villa. <i>When The Lion Feeds</i> was my introduction to this most diligent of writers. I was immediately immersed in historical fiction the like of which I have never experienced throughout the thirty or more years since that holiday.<br/><br/>I followed the Courtneys and the Ballant</span>
  <span id="freeText18402071591851339067" style="display:none">I was first introduced to Wilbur Smith’s writing several years ago while laid up with my leg in plaster halfway through a holiday in Lanzarote. I needed something to read, and there were several paperbacks left behind in our rented villa. <i>When The Lion Feeds</i> was my introduction to this most diligent of writers. I was immediately immersed in historical fiction the like of which I have never experienced throughout the thirty or more years since that holiday.<br /><br />I followed the Courtneys and the Ballantynes, and then came <i>River God</i>... Well! I was stunned by an epic tale revealing the sweeping grandeur of Ancient Egypt, and narrated with the same authority and attention to detail that I had already encountered in at least a dozen other of Smith’s novels. I was captivated by the character of the eunuch Taita. So much so, that I nearly found myself checking through history books to see if Smith’s story and characters had any basis in fact... (I’ll leave you to make your own enquiries!)<br /><br />And then along came a sort of sequel that I was not expecting: <i>The Seventh Scroll</i>.<br /><br />Instead of giving us another adventure with Taita, Smith presents us with a modern day treasure hunt, but with the <i>River God</i> story acting as the map! Immediately I found myself returning to the question: was Wilbur Smith’s remarkable first venture into Egyptian history simply a work of fiction? The pursuit of the truth (and the treasure) is immaculately portrayed, tantalising the reader like a set of hieroglyphs that are both beautiful in presentation, and mysterious in their translation. It would be impossible not to draw a comparison here with the writing of Henry Rider Haggard, and Smith’s hero (Nicholas Quenton-Harper) does have more than a passing resemblance to Allan Quartermain, but I’m not complaining. While Haggard’s stories may have been original, his storylines had more place in comic book fiction. On the other hand Smith manages to take us into a real place, and even the peculiarities of his art-collecting villain manage to avoid the implausible scenarios of a fantasy akin to the likes of Indiana Jones.<br /><br />Whether you enjoy adventure or romance, mystery or thriller, historical fiction or travel guides, there’s something here for you! Wilbur Smith is unrivalled in painting on a broad canvas, and his artistry at bringing Africa to life in book form has never been beaten. In <i>The Seventh Scroll</i> he has added an extra mixture of intrigue and suspense that takes his writing to the highest level. What is there not to like?<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="18402071591851339067" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/255203454" />
    <a title="Alex" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4883137-alex"><img alt="Alex" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1340197207p2/4883137.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/255203454?book_show_action=true">Jan 02, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alex" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alex" href="/user/show/4883137-alex">Alex</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780333583210" href="/book/show/906598.The_Seventh_Scroll">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4883137-alex?shelf=2012-books">2012-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer255203454" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3281294753912512268">Website | Goodreads | Twitter | Facebook<br/>Publication Date: November 10, 1995<br/>Publisher: MacMillian<br/>Country: United States of America<br/>ISBN: 0333637704<br/>Original Language: English<br/>Age Group: Adult<br/>Genre: Historical, Thriller<br/>Buy the Book: Amazon<br/>Lootability: Grab it and go<br/><br/>The Seventh Scroll is Wilbur Smith's 25th book and the second in his popular Egyptian series featuring the enigmatic slave Taita. A modern look at the events of River God, The Seventh Scroll follows the archaeological rediscovery of Pharao</span>
  <span id="freeText3281294753912512268" style="display:none">Website | Goodreads | Twitter | Facebook<br />Publication Date: November 10, 1995<br />Publisher: MacMillian<br />Country: United States of America<br />ISBN: 0333637704<br />Original Language: English<br />Age Group: Adult<br />Genre: Historical, Thriller<br />Buy the Book: Amazon<br />Lootability: Grab it and go<br /><br />The Seventh Scroll is Wilbur Smith's 25th book and the second in his popular Egyptian series featuring the enigmatic slave Taita. A modern look at the events of River God, The Seventh Scroll follows the archaeological rediscovery of Pharaoh Mamose's tomb. <br /><br />What I liked: Taita was such an enthralling protagonist in River God and it was a pleasure to revisit him. It was cleverly designed for us to see the results of Taita's work instead of hearing him boast about his genius - and we get to delight and despair with Nicholas and Royan as they play his game and struggle to discover the location of Mamose's tomb. <br />I also like the way that Smith teases himself as writing books full of "sex and violence" who takes liberties with history. <br />Like in River God, Smith creates a believable world of Egyptian archeology and, from what I remember of my ancient history lessons, seems to recreate the culture beautifully. <br /><br />What I didn't like: There really is a lot of sex and violence in Wilbur Smith's books. It's for that reason that I can't reread the third book in series, Warlock. Sometimes it just becomes overwhelming - too many people are tortured, injured or killed carelessly for my tastes. <br />Some of the phrasing seems odd to me, a lot of extra justs and verys.<br /><br />There are another two books in the series, featuring Taita and returning to ancient times. However, from memory I found the story-lines rather violent and far-fetched. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3281294753912512268" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26654708-chakib-bahbaz?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer993937549&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer993937549" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"

<span id="freeTextContainer12731379447307048359">4.5/5<br/><br/>This is by far the best thing i have ever read , The Seventh Scroll by Wilbur Smith is a historical-fiction novel and the second book in the Ancient Egypt series , the story is a non-stop action that makes your brain evaporates from imagination and in the same time makes you pop your eyes out stick your fingers in their sockets and swirl your brain around ....<br/><br/>First i though that the writer Includes additional , yet unnecessary details and in more than one occasion i lost interest in finishi</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer3679561644850626345">A bit like Alfred Hitchcock, Wilbur Smith occasionally drops in for a moment or two as a cameo character. <br/><br/>Royan Al Simma and her husband have dedicated themselves to unravelling the secrets of the Seventh Scroll, a cryptic four-thousand year old document, full of riddles and clues to the last resting place of the Pharaoh Mamose. A treasure trove awaits anyone who can decipher the enigmatic puns and misdirections of the self-proclaimed genius, the slave Taita. And that treasure is enough to tempt</span>
  <span id="freeText3679561644850626345" style="display:none">A bit like Alfred Hitchcock, Wilbur Smith occasionally drops in for a moment or two as a cameo character. <br /><br />Royan Al Simma and her husband have dedicated themselves to unravelling the secrets of the Seventh Scroll, a cryptic four-thousand year old document, full of riddles and clues to the last resting place of the Pharaoh Mamose. A treasure trove awaits anyone who can decipher the enigmatic puns and misdirections of the self-proclaimed genius, the slave Taita. And that treasure is enough to tempt not only those desiring wealth but also those obsessed with making an immortal name for themselves.<br /><br />Royan's husband is murdered and, with his dying breath, he directs her to seek out Nicholas Quenton-Harper, a titled Englishman and skilled amateur archaeologist, who has the means to help her in the search for the Pharaoh's tomb. Or rather, "had the means". The estate is in some financial difficulty.<br /><br />But Nicholas becomes intrigued enough by the attempts on Royan's life to think that perhaps there is some truth in her strange story of a tomb near the source of the Nile. As it happens, he was once in the very gorge and has an intimate knowledge of the difficulties of the terrain. And he has the perfect excuse to go back: he has to regain the family honour. One of his ancestors has been long accused of faking a particular species of dik-dik. The one and only specimen in the family's collection has been regarded as fraudulent for a century. So it's off they go, in the wilds of Ethiopia, allegedly dik-dik hunting... a ruse that should put the killers off the scent. <br /><br />But which fools no one.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3679561644850626345" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer3827928631124241067">The premise of the book is, I thought, unusual although there may be other books that use the same approach. The characters in 'The Seventh Scroll' have read the novel written by author Wilbur Smith, River God, and believing the Egyptian characters, and the buried treasure to be real, set out to find it after four thousand years. <br/><br/>Of course, I read 'River God' and loved it, but never for a moment believed it to be real. But who knows? Wilbur Smith manages to write another terrific blockbuster bas</span>
  <span id="freeText3827928631124241067" style="display:none">The premise of the book is, I thought, unusual although there may be other books that use the same approach. The characters in 'The Seventh Scroll' have read the novel written by author Wilbur Smith, River God, and believing the Egyptian characters, and the buried treasure to be real, set out to find it after four thousand years. <br /><br />Of course, I read 'River God' and loved it, but never for a moment believed it to be real. But who knows? Wilbur Smith manages to write another terrific blockbuster based on the modern day fiction characters, Royan and Sir Nicholas. They are determined to find the four thousand year old treasure. There is competition once it is suggested that they might know what they are doing and as usual there are casualties of war. <br /><br />Not only did I love this book, it also made me want to re-read the book River God, which luckily I still have on my bookshelf! Nevertheless I still have quite a few books that Smith has written that I have not read. Some I still have not yet found, but I am determined to do so, not unlike the characters of this book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3827928631124241067" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer17381810513425520231">This book is based on a previous book, which I hadn't actually read. However, I don't think it's actually necessary to have read the previous book, unless you are interested in the fictional historical references about the places they're visiting and the items they're looking for.<br/><br/>This book, I felt, was completely full of ridiculous ideas. For me, that wouldn't be so bad if it was obvious from the start that it was an overblown and obviously fictitious idea of what's possible; I've read enough fa</span>
  <span id="freeText17381810513425520231" style="display:none">This book is based on a previous book, which I hadn't actually read. However, I don't think it's actually necessary to have read the previous book, unless you are interested in the fictional historical references about the places they're visiting and the items they're looking for.<br /><br />This book, I felt, was completely full of ridiculous ideas. For me, that wouldn't be so bad if it was obvious from the start that it was an overblown and obviously fictitious idea of what's possible; I've read enough fantasy to cope with worlds based on reality but in which things are performed that wouldn't be possible within the real world. Like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/14975.Labyrinth__Languedoc___1_" title="Labyrinth (Languedoc, #1) by Kate Mosse" rel="nofollow noopener">Labyrinth</a>, for example - it's historical fiction with the emphasis on the fiction - at no point does <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/9343.Kate_Mosse" title="Kate Mosse" rel="nofollow noopener">Kate Mosse</a> try to pretend that it's anything else (plus she does better research). Yet here... I had the feeling that he was pushing at that boundary, especially considering he quite happily inserted himself into the text, by name, as a purported "expert". If there's <i>anything</i> which gives you the wrong impression of the author, it's that.<br /><br />I'd read one of his other books previously, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/37619.Warlock_A_Novel_of_Ancient_Egypt__Ancient_Egypt___3_" title="Warlock A Novel of Ancient Egypt (Ancient Egypt, #3) by Wilbur A. Smith" rel="nofollow noopener">Warlock: A Novel of Ancient Egypt</a>, which I thought was OK and relatively interesting. But after reading this book it's put me off his works for life.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17381810513425520231" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer8737243516334961515">I picked this up thinking it would be a mix of Clive Cussler and other adventure history type books. I am obssessed with Egyptian mysteries so was very excited when I first started on this. While it was action packed (with all of the fighting and gruesome details it had concerning torture) I found it considerably lacking. It didn't have the intricate detail of Cussler nor did it have excitement that swept me up and caused me to look forward to reading it. The descriptions of ancient Egyptian myt</span>
  <span id="freeText8737243516334961515" style="display:none">I picked this up thinking it would be a mix of Clive Cussler and other adventure history type books. I am obssessed with Egyptian mysteries so was very excited when I first started on this. While it was action packed (with all of the fighting and gruesome details it had concerning torture) I found it considerably lacking. It didn't have the intricate detail of Cussler nor did it have excitement that swept me up and caused me to look forward to reading it. The descriptions of ancient Egyptian mythology were wonderful and accurate but descriptions of landmarks and the landscape as a whole were lacking. Imagining exactly where they were in the world and what they were doing was tricky at best and downright confusing at other times. All in all, I don't think I would read his books as opposed to Clive Cussler's. </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer11737256914202607626">As the second book of Wilbur Smith's Ancient Egypt trilogy, this book was somewhat disappointing. Most of the setting is modern-day Ethiopia where the two main characters search for the tomb and treasure told about in River God <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/429138.River_God__Ancient_Egypt___1_' title='River God (Ancient Egypt, #1) by Wilbur Smith' rel='nofollow noopener'>River God</a>, the first book of the trilogy.   <span id="freeText11737256914202607626" style="display:none">As the second book of Wilbur Smith's Ancient Egypt trilogy, this book was somewhat disappointing. Most of the setting is modern-day Ethiopia where the two main characters search for the tomb and treasure told about in River God <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/429138.River_God__Ancient_Egypt___1_" title="River God (Ancient Egypt, #1) by Wilbur Smith" rel="nofollow noopener">River God</a>, the first book of the trilogy. I really enjoyed River God, but this book, with the exception of a few exciting sections, was slow-moving. Another dissatisfying aspect of the book was the dialogue between the two main characters, Royan and Nicholas. No matter how traumatic or dangerous their experiences were they seemed to be able to make only facetious remarks to each other. What I did like about the book was the way Smith tied together the story lines from episodes four thousand years apart. </span>

<div id="review_16867532" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/16867532" />
    <a title="Angela" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/797370-angela"><img alt="Angela" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/16867532?book_show_action=true">Mar 02, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Angela" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Angela" href="/user/show/797370-angela">Angela</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780312119997" href="/book/show/1316901.The_Seventh_Scroll">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer16867532&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer16867532" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8615062162285372885">This is a book that makes you wish you were an archeologist. If you have read RiverGod, this is the next book that you should read. In RiverGod, the story is told by Taita an Egyptian slave. He tells you about his life with Lostris, who becomes Pharoah's wife, and Tanus, who is Lostris' childhood love. <br/>In The Seventh Scroll, the story is about 2 archeologists who find the tombs of Lostris' husband Pharoah and that of Tanus. And the artwork of Taita.<br/>A good story. <br/><br/></span>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_631635298" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/631635298" />
    <a title="Sumeetha Manikandan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14638199-sumeetha-manikandan"><img alt="Sumeetha Manikandan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442893618p2/14638199.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/631635298?book_show_action=true">Jun 03, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sumeetha Manikandan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sumeetha Manikandan" href="/user/show/14638199-sumeetha-manikandan">Sumeetha Manikandan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer631635298" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1117595553305313733">Half way through this book, I went back to my lending library and got the River God (first part) again. Though Seventh Scroll can be read individually too, I wanted to read River God again.<br/><br/>In Seventh Scroll, we see an archeologist and an adventurer trying their best to decipher the scrolls of slave Taita. It also brings home the fact that how history is distorted and presented as fact. This was a nice adventure book that I enjoyed immensely. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('X4vjSX+XnMZNH1MlBcD38oDfk0Y4MYOX8R38FzP6krnFsRewCcllUlN7+m3Xqt5SLIJeKziAYCbM3FZ+UFf5TQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('+17p1WroVz4zF7pF40EAUTDW6SvliFVLEjM3HVzsjd5hZB0sHLauqi1zEw0xKynxnIskRuU5tvov8p10P0HmKg==')})
  }
//]]>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer14426651097098832144">Wilbur Smith is the bestselling author of many novels, each meticulously researched on his numerous expeditions worldwide. His bestselling Courtney series includes <i>Assegai</i>, <i>The Sound of Thunder</i>, <i>Birds of Prey</i>, <i>Monsoon</i>, and <i>Blue Horizon</i>. His other books include <i>Those in Peril</i>, <i>River God</i>, <i>Warlock</i>, <i>The Seventh Scroll</i>, and <i>The Sunbird</i>. His books are now translated into twenty-six languages and have so</span>
  <span id="freeText14426651097098832144" style="display:none">Wilbur Smith is the bestselling author of many novels, each meticulously researched on his numerous expeditions worldwide. His bestselling Courtney series includes <i>Assegai</i>, <i>The Sound of Thunder</i>, <i>Birds of Prey</i>, <i>Monsoon</i>, and <i>Blue Horizon</i>. His other books include <i>Those in Peril</i>, <i>River God</i>, <i>Warlock</i>, <i>The Seventh Scroll</i>, and <i>The Sunbird</i>. His books are now translated into twenty-six languages and have sold over 120 million copies. Smith was born to a British family in Northern Rhodesia, now Zambia, in Central Africa, and attended Rhodes University in South Africa. He has homes in Cape Town, London, Switzerland and Malta.</span>
